Nikah — Marriage

8 min
Validity of Dhimmi Witnesses in Muslim-Dhimmi Marriage
Explaining the validity of a Muslim marrying a dhimmi woman with dhimmi witnesses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf versus Muhammad al-Shaybani on the validity of a Muslim marrying a dhimmi woman with dhimmi witnesses, and explain the reasoning behind each position.

Core Idea
When the wife is a dhimmi, dhimmi witnesses are valid – testimony is against her, not the Muslim husband.

This passage reveals a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of dhimmi witnesses in a Muslim-dhimmi marriage. While Muhammad al-Shaybani rejects it based on the principle that a disbeliever's testimony is inadmissible against a Muslim, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf argue that the testimony is against the dhimmi wife, not the Muslim husband. Understanding this distinction clarifies when the condition of Islam for witnesses applies.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi Majority Position
Identify what Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold: a Muslim man may validly marry a dhimmi woman with two dhimmi witnesses.
This is the mufta bihi view.
2
Identify the Opponent and Their View
Muhammad al-Shaybani opposes: he considers the marriage invalid because dhimmi witnesses cannot testify against a Muslim.
His objection is based on the nature of hearing as testimony.
3
Follow the Opponent's Proof
Muhammad argues that hearing the offer and acceptance in marriage constitutes testimony (shahadah). A disbeliever's testimony against a Muslim is invalid, so it is as if no witnesses were present.
4
Analyse the Hanafi Majority Response
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf respond: testimony in marriage is for establishing ownership (milk) over the wife, not for monetary claims. The witnesses testify against the dhimmi woman, not the Muslim man. Therefore, their dhimmi status does not disqualify them.
They also note that the condition of Islam for witnesses only applies when both spouses are Muslim.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: if a marriage is performed with dhimmi witnesses, it is valid according to the majority but invalid according to Muhammad. The fatwa is given on the majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وصح تزوج مسلم ذمية عند ذميين بيان لكون اشتراط إسلام الشاهد إنما هو إذا كانا مسلمين، أما إذا كانت ذمية فلا عندهما. وقال محمد : لا يجوز لأن السماع في النكاح شهادة ولا شهادة للكافر على المسلم فكأنهما لم يسمعا كلام المسلم ولهما أن الشهادة شرطت في النكاح على اعتبار إثبات الملك لوروده على محل ذي خطر لا على اعتبار وجوب المهر إذ لا شهادة تشترط في لزوم المال وهما شاهدان عليها بخلاف ما إذا لم يسمعا كلامه لأن العقد ينعقد بكلاميهما والشهادة شرط على العقد أطلق في الذميين فشمل ما إذا كانا موافقين لها في الملة أو مخالفين كذا في البدائع. وقيد بصحة العقد لأن أداءهما عند القاضي عند إنكار المسلم غير صحيح إجماعاً وعند إنكارها مقبول عندهما مطلقاً، وعند محمد إن قالا كان معنا مسلمان وقت العقد قبل وإلا فلا وكذا إذا أسلما وأديا فعلى هذا الخلاف. كذا في شرح الطحاوي. وعن محمد لا تقبل شهادتهما مطلقاً . قال في البدائع: وهو الصحيح من مذهبه لأنها قامت على إثبات فعل المسلم على نكاح فاسد
His statement: (It is valid for a Muslim to marry a dhimmi woman in the presence of two dhimmis) is an explanation that the condition of the witness's Islam is only when both are Muslims. As for when the woman is a dhimmi, then it is valid according to them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). Muhammad said: It is not permissible because hearing in marriage is testimony, and there is no testimony of a disbeliever against a Muslim, so it is as if they did not hear the Muslim's speech. Their (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's) argument: Testimony is required in marriage for the purpose of establishing ownership because it pertains to a matter of importance, not for the purpose of assessing the dower, since no testimony is required for the necessity of wealth, and they are witnesses against her. Unlike if they did not hear his speech because the contract is concluded by their speech, and testimony is a condition for the contract. It is general regarding dhimmis, including whether they are of the same religion or different. So in al-Bada'i. It is restricted to the validity of the contract because their testimony before the judge if the Muslim denies is not valid by consensus, and if she denies, it is accepted absolutely according to them. According to Muhammad, if they say 'there were two Muslims with us at the time of the contract' it is accepted, otherwise not. Similarly if they become Muslim and testify, then the same disagreement. So in Sharh al-Tahawi. And from Muhammad: their testimony is not accepted at all. Al-Bada'i said: That is the correct view of his school because it is based on establishing the act of a Muslim regarding a null marriage.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage details an intra-Hanafi dispute. The base ruling is that a Muslim man may validly marry a dhimmi woman in the presence of two dhimmi witnesses. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold this as valid, while Muhammad al-Shaybani rejects it. Muhammad's reasoning: hearing the marriage proposal and acceptance constitutes testimony, and a disbeliever's testimony against a Muslim is invalid, so the witnesses are as if they didn't hear. The two imams argue that testimony in marriage is required for establishing ownership, not for monetary obligations; the witnesses are testifying against the woman, not the man. They also allow it whether the witnesses share the woman's religion or not. The text notes that if the Muslim husband denies the marriage, the dhimmi witnesses cannot testify against him by consensus; but if the wife denies, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf accept their testimony absolutely, while Muhammad only accepts if they say Muslims were present. Al-Bada'i states that Muhammad's absolute rejection is the correct view of his school. The mufta bihi is the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (validity).

Key Terms
ذمية (dhimmīyah): A non-Muslim woman living under Muslim rule with a pact of protection (dhimmah).
سماع (samā‘): Hearing the proposal and acceptance in a marriage contract; considered testimony according to Muhammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, why are dhimmi witnesses acceptable when a Muslim marries a dhimmi woman?
A) Because the witnesses must be of the same religion as the wife
B) Because testimony is about establishing ownership, not about the man's obligation
C) Because dhimmi witnesses are always acceptable in any marriage
D) Because the man is also a dhimmi

Key Takeaway
A Muslim man's marriage to a dhimmi woman with dhimmi witnesses is valid according to the majority Hanafi view because the condition of Islam for witnesses applies only when both spouses are Muslim.

Nikah — Marriage

8 min
Father Ordering Marriage of Minor Daughter: Validity and Witness Role
Intra-Hanafi dispute on validity of marriage by agent for minor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement on the validity of a marriage contract for a minor when the father is absent, and distinguish the practical consequences (thamarat) of the two positions.

Core Idea
Father must be present; agent is only a messenger, not a contractor.

In Hanafi marriage law, a father can appoint an agent to marry his minor daughter. A key question arises: can the father be absent and still be considered a direct contractor or witness? The relied-upon view requires the father's presence, but a view in al-Nihayah permits it. This lesson examines the reasoning, the point of disagreement, and how this difference affects witness requirements and later testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
State the ruling: the marriage is valid if the father is present at the contract; otherwise it is invalid. This is the relied-upon (mu'tamad) view.
The condition is the father's presence, not just his consent.
2
Understand the legal reasoning
Why is the father considered the direct contractor? Because of the unity of the assembly (ittihad al-majlis) – the agent is only a messenger (safir) and the one who utters the contract (muzawwaj) remains a witness.
The agent's words are directly attributed to the father.
3
Identify the point of disagreement
The view in al-Nihayah holds that the father can be a witness even when absent, because the agent is a messenger and the contract can be attributed to the father without his physical presence.
This is an intra-Hanafi difference; both sides are from within the school.
4
Analyze the practical consequences (thamarat)
Two consequences: (1) If the agent is a woman, the relied-upon view requires two women witnesses; al-Nihayah accepts one man. (2) If the father later testifies about the marriage after the daughter reaches puberty and she denies, the relied-upon view rejects his testimony (he is considered a contractor), while al-Nihayah accepts it (he is a witness).
These show how the theoretical difference impacts real cases.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The author (al-Bahr al-Raiq) prefers the relied-upon view, stating 'هذا المعتمد' (this is the relied-upon).
Tarjih is based on the principle of making the father the direct contractor only when he is physically present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومن أمر رجلاً أن يزوج صغيرته فزوجها عند رجل والأب حاضر صح وإلا فلا) لأن الأب يجعل مباشراً للعقد باتحاد المجلس ليكون الوكيل سفيراً ومعبراً فبقي المزوج شاهداً، وإن كان الأب غائباً لم يجز لأن المجلس مختلف فلا يمكن أن يجعل الأب مباشراً وهذا المعتمد خلافاً لما في النهاية من إمكان جعل الأب شاهداً من غير نقل عبارة الوكيل إليه، ولم أر من نبه على ثمرة هذا الاختلاف، وقد ظهر لي أن ثمرته في موضعين : الأول أن وكيل الأب لو كان امرأة فعلى المعتمد لا ينعقد بحضور رجل بل لا بد من امرأة أخرى، وعلى ما في النهاية ينعقد. ولو كان الآمر بتزويج الصغيرة أمها انعكس الحكم الثاني لو شهد الأب بالنكاح بعد بلوغها وهي تنكر فعلى طريقة ما في النهاية ينبغي أن تقبل لأنه شاهد لا مزوج، وعلى المعتمد لا تقبل لأنه مزوج. ولو كان الآمر الأخ أو العم فشهد لها أو عليها فعلى ما في النهاية تقبل، وعلى المعتمد لا تقبل فليتأمل. وعبارة النقاية هنا أخصر وأفود حيث قال: والوكيل شاهد إن حضر موكله كالولي إن حضرت موليته بالغة اهـ. ولأنه لا فرق بين أن يكون المأمور رجلاً أو امرأة، فإن كان رجلاً اشترط أن يكون معه رجل آخر أو امرأتان، وإن كان امرأة اشترط أن يكون معها رجلان أو رجل وامرأة وبه علم أن قوله «عند رجل» ليس بقيد لأن المرأتين كذلك. وقيد بكون المولية بالغة لأنها لو كانت صغيرة لا يكون الولي شاهداً لأن العقد لا يمكن نقله إليها. وعلى هذا فلا حاجة إلى قوله «كالولي» لأنه في هذه الحالة وكيل فدخل تحت الأول. وقيد بحضرة موكله لأنه لو وكل المولى رجلاً في تزويج عبده فزوجه الوكيل بشهادة واحد والعبد حاضر لم يجز لأن العقد لم ينتقل إليه لعدم التوكيل من جهته، وإن أذن لعبده أن يتزوج فتزوج بشهادة المولى ورجل آخر فالصواب أنه يجوز ويكون المولى شاهداً لأن العبد يتصرف بأهلية نفسه والإذن فك الحجر وليس بتوكيل. المهر من بأي وجه أمكن. وعليه لا وجه لقوله ولم أر من نبه الخ وعليك أن تتأمل ذلك اهـ. قوله : (خلافاً لما في النهاية) قال في الحواشي السعدية يؤيد كلام صاحب النهاية ما سيجيء في الهداية في باب أن الولي في تزويج الصغيرة سفير ومعبر لا عاقد مباشر فراجعه قوله: (ولو كان الأمر بتزويج الصغيرة أمها انعكس الحكم قال الرملي : وفي نسخة ولو كان الآمر بتزويج الصغيرة امرأة والمأمور رجلاً انعكس الحكم قوله : ( وعلى المعتمد لا تقبل لأنه مزوج قال الرملي : قد يقال جعله مزوجاً لضرورة تصحيح النكاح وما ثبت بالضرورة يتقدر بقدرها. وأيضاً على ما في النهاية جعله شاهداً للضرورة، والذي ينبغي قبول شهادته لأنه لم يتول التزويج بنفسه فبقي مجرد الحضور حقيقة فتقبل عليها لا لها وإن قيل بعدم القبول لكون الوكيل في النكاح سفيراً ومعبراً فيثبت نقله إلى الموكل فله وجه فتأمل. وراجع النقل فلعلك تظفر بالمسألة قوله : (وعلى المعتمد لا تقبل) قال النهر : يعني إذا قال أنا زوجتها أما إذا قال هذه زوجته قبلت .
His statement: (If a man orders another to marry his minor daughter, and he marries her in the presence of a man and the father is present, it is valid; otherwise not) because the father is made the direct contractor by the unity of the assembly so that the agent becomes a messenger and interpreter, and the person who conducted the marriage remains a witness. If the father is absent, it is not valid because the assembly is different and the father cannot be made the direct contractor. This is the relied-upon view, contrary to what is in al-Nihayah that it is possible to make the father a witness without transferring the agent's words. I have not seen anyone point out the practical consequence of this difference. It has become clear to me that its consequence is in two places: first, if the father's agent is a woman, then according to the relied-upon view, it does not take place with one man present; rather another woman is needed. According to al-Nihayah, it does take place. Second, if the mother is the one ordering the marriage of the minor, the ruling is reversed. Second, if the father testifies to the marriage after she reaches puberty and she denies, then according to the method of al-Nihayah, it should be accepted because he is a witness, not a contractor. According to the relied-upon view, it is not accepted because he is a contractor. If the brother or uncle is the one ordering, and he testifies for or against, then according to al-Nihayah it is accepted, and according to the relied-upon view it is not accepted; let one contemplate. The expression of al-Niqayah here is more concise and beneficial where it said: The agent is a witness if his principal is present, like the guardian if his female ward who is of age is present. End. And because there is no difference whether the person ordered is a man or a woman. If it is a man, it is required that there be with him another man or two women. If it is a woman, it is required that there be with her two men or a man and a woman. By this it is known that his saying 'in the presence of a man' is not a restriction because two women are similar. It is restricted to the ward being of age because if she were a minor, the guardian would not be a witness because the contract cannot be transferred to her. Accordingly, there is no need for his saying 'like the guardian' because in this case he is an agent and falls under the first. It is restricted to the presence of his principal because if a guardian authorizes a man to marry his slave, and the agent marries him with one witness while the slave is present, it is not permissible because the contract is not transferred to him due to lack of authorization from his side. If he permits his slave to marry, and he marries with the testimony of the master and another man, the correct view is that it is permissible and the master is a witness because the slave acts with his own capacity and permission is removal of the restriction, not authorization. The dower is from any possible means. Accordingly, there is no basis for his statement 'I have not seen anyone point out' and you should contemplate that. End. His statement: (Contrary to what is in al-Nihayah) said in al-Hawashi al-Sa'diyyah: The statement of the author of al-Nihayah is supported by what will come in al-Hidayah in the chapter that the guardian in marrying a minor is a messenger and interpreter, not a direct contractor; so refer to it. His statement: (If the mother is the one ordering the marriage of the minor, the ruling is reversed) al-Ramli said: In another copy, if the one ordering is a woman and the one ordered is a man, the ruling is reversed. His statement: (According to the relied-upon view, it is not accepted because he is a contractor) al-Ramli said: It may be said that making him a contractor is out of necessity to validate the marriage, and what is established by necessity is limited to its extent. Also, according to al-Nihayah he is made a witness out of necessity. What is appropriate is acceptance of his testimony because he did not perform the marriage himself; he remained mere presence in reality, so it is accepted against her, not for her. Even if it is said that it is not accepted because the agent in marriage is a messenger and interpreter, so transfer to the principal is established; thus there is a possibility; so contemplate. Refer to the transmission; perhaps you will come across the issue. His statement: (According to the relied-upon view, it is not accepted) al-Nahr said: That is if he says 'I married her'; but if he says 'this is his wife', it is accepted.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of a marriage contract when a father orders another person to marry his minor daughter. The base ruling is that if the father is present at the contract, the marriage is valid; if absent, it is not. The reason: the father is considered the direct contractor due to the unity of the assembly, and the agent is only a messenger. The person who performed the verbal contract remains a witness. The relied-upon view (al-mu'tamad) states that when the father is absent, the assembly is different, so the father cannot be direct, and the marriage is invalid. This differs from the view in al-Nihayah, which allows the father to be considered a witness even without transferring the agent's words. The author then elaborates on two practical consequences (thamarat) of this difference: (1) if the agent is a woman, the relied-upon view requires another woman as a witness, while al-Nihayah's view accepts it; (2) if the father later testifies about the marriage after the daughter reaches puberty and she denies, the relied-upon view rejects his testimony because he is considered a contractor, while al-Nihayah accepts it as he is a witness. The commentary also discusses cases where the mother, brother, or uncle orders the marriage, and whether the agent's gender affects the requirement for witnesses. The passage concludes with a note on the testimony of the father regarding the marriage.

Key Terms
مزوج (muzawwaj): The person who performs the marriage contract verbally (the one who says 'zawwajtu').
سفير (safīr): A messenger who simply conveys the words of the principal without being the direct contractor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view, if a father orders an agent to marry his minor daughter, what is required for the marriage to be valid?
A) The agent must be a male
B) The father must be present at the contract
C) Two female witnesses must be present
D) The contract must be written

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi view requires the father's presence for a valid marriage of his minor daughter via an agent; the view in al-Nihayah differs, affecting witness requirements and later testimony.

Definition of Mahram and the Nine Categories of Prohibited Women
Definition and enumeration of mahram categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the nine categories of women permanently prohibited for marriage in the Hanafi school and identify which cause applies to a given scenario.

Core Idea
A mahram is a woman permanently haram to marry due to lineage, marriage alliance, breastfeeding, or other specific legal causes.

Marriage in Islam is a sacred contract, but not every woman is eligible. The law defines certain women as 'mahram'—permanently prohibited to marry. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq enumerates nine such causes, from blood relations to the effect of triple divorce. Understanding these categories is essential for every Muslim to ensure valid marriages.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of mahram
The passage defines mahram as a woman who is permanently prohibited for marriage due to a legal cause. Note that the prohibition is actual (haqiqa), not merely apparent.
The term 'mahram' is sometimes used for a male guardian, but here it refers to a woman prohibited for marriage.
2
Identify the nine causes of prohibition
Read through the list: (1) lineage, (2) marriage alliance, (3) breastfeeding, (4) combining mahrams/strangers, (5) precedence of free over slave, (6) right of another, (7) lack of a revealed religion, (8) conflict (master-slave), (9) triple divorce. Understand each cause and its scope.
The ninth cause (triple divorce) is mentioned but is often discussed separately in the chapter on rights of others.
3
Differentiate permanent vs. temporary prohibition
Mahram is a permanent prohibition. Temporary prohibitions (e.g., sister-in-law while wife is alive, or a married woman) are not considered mahram. In this list, #6 includes temporary prohibitions due to rights of others, but the passage classifies them as part of the nine. Recognize that some of these are permanent (lineage) while others may be temporary (e.g., married woman until divorce/iddah).
The author includes 'right of another' as a category, but those women are not permanently mahram after the obstacle is removed.
4
Note the dispute on the placement of the ninth category
Al-Zayla'i and many others omit triple divorce from this list, considering it as part of the right of another. The author of al-Kanz mentions seven categories here and puts triple divorce in a later section. Understand that this is a matter of arrangement, not substance.
The mufta bihi is that triple divorce is a separate cause, but practically it is always about the right of another (the first husband's right to remarry after tahlil).
5
Apply to a scenario
Given a proposed marriage, check each of the nine categories. For example, if a man wants to marry his foster sister (breastfeeding), it is prohibited under category 3. If he wants to marry a Magian woman, it is prohibited under category 7. Practice identifying the relevant cause.
Always verify that the cause is permanent. Some categories (like right of another) may cease after the obstacle is removed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمحرم حقيقة الفعل، ورجحوا أنه حقيقة وانتفاء محلية المرأة للنكاح شرعاً بأسباب تسعة : الأول المحرمات بالنسب وهن فروعه وأصوله وفروع أبويه وإن نزلوا وفروع أجداده وجداته إذا انفصلوا ببطن واحد. الثاني المحرمات بالمصاهرة وهن فروع نسائه المدخول بهن وأصولهن وحلائل فروعه وحلائل أصوله والثالث المحرمات بالرضاع وأنواعهن كالنسب. والرابع حرمة الجمع بين المحارم وحرمة الجمع بين الأجنبيات كالجمع بين الخمس. والخامس حرمة التقديم وهو تقديم الحرة على الأمة جعله في النهاية والمحيط قسماً على حدة، وأدخله الزيلعي في حرمة الجمع فقال : وحرمة الجمع بين الحرة والأمة والحرة متقدمة. وهو الأنسب . والسادس المحرمة لحق الغير كمنكوحة الغير ومعتدته والحامل بثابت النسب. والسابع المحرمة لعدم دين سماوي كالمجوسية والمشركة والثامن المحرمة للتنافي كنكاح السيدة مملوكها . والتاسع لم يذكره الزيلعي وكثير وهو المحرمة بالطلقات الثلاث ذكره في المحيط والنهاية. وقد ذكر المصنف في هذا الفصل سبعة منها وذكر المحرمة بالطلقات الثلاث في فصل من بالحرمة لحق الغير لظهوره
The mahram is the actual act, and they preferred that it is actual, and it is the negation of the woman's eligibility for marriage legally due to nine causes: First: those prohibited by lineage, and they are his descendants and his ascendants and the descendants of his parents, even if they go down, and the descendants of his grandparents, if they are separated by one belly. Second: those prohibited by marriage alliance, and they are the descendants of his wives with whom he has consummated, and their ascendants, and the wives of his descendants, and the wives of his ascendants. Third: those prohibited by breastfeeding, and their types are like lineage. Fourth: prohibition of combining between mahrams and prohibition of combining between non-mahram women like combining five. Fifth: prohibition of precedence, which is giving precedence to a free woman over a slave woman. End of the book and the surrounding made it a separate section, and al-Zayla'i included it in the prohibition of combining, saying: 'And the prohibition of combining between a free woman and a slave woman, and the free woman takes precedence.' And that is more appropriate. Sixth: prohibited due to the right of another, like another's married woman and his woman in 'iddah and the pregnant woman with established lineage. Seventh: prohibited due to lack of a revealed religion, like the Magian and the polytheist woman. Eighth: prohibited due to conflict, like the marriage of a female master to her slave. Ninth: al-Zayla'i and many did not mention it, and it is the woman prohibited by triple divorce, mentioned in al-Muhit and al-Nihaya. And the author mentioned in this chapter seven of them, and mentioned the woman prohibited by triple divorce in the chapter of those prohibited by the right of another due to its clarity.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a definition and enumeration ruling. It defines 'mahram' as a woman who is permanently prohibited for marriage due to a specific legal cause. The author clarifies that the ruling is actual (haqiqa), not merely apparent. He then lists nine categories of such prohibition: (1) lineage: includes all descendants and ascendants of the man and his parents and grandparents, provided they are connected by a single belly (i.e., no separation of generation through adoption or the like). (2) marriage alliance: includes the descendants and ascendants of wives with whom consummation occurred, and the spouses of his own descendants and ascendants. (3) breastfeeding: the same categories as lineage apply through fosterage. (4) combining mahrams: e.g., marrying two sisters simultaneously. (5) precedence of a free woman over a slave woman when combining them. (6) rights of others: e.g., a married woman or a woman in 'iddah. (7) lack of a divine religion: a Magian or polytheist woman. (8) conflict: e.g., a female master marrying her own slave. (9) triple divorce: a woman who has been divorced three times is prohibited to the divorcer until she marries another man. The author of the matn (al-Kanz) mentioned only seven of these in this chapter, deferring the ninth to a later section on 'prohibition due to the right of another.' This division is a matter of arrangement, not substance.

Key Terms
محرم (mahram): A woman who is permanently prohibited for marriage due to a specific legal cause (lineage, marriage alliance, breastfeeding, etc.).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a valid category of permanent prohibition (mahram) according to the Hanafi list?
A) A woman who is engaged to another man
B) A woman who is a maternal cousin
C) A woman who is the foster mother (who breastfed him)
D) A woman who is his neighbor

Key Takeaway
The nine categories of mahram women are: lineage, marriage alliance, breastfeeding, combining (mahrams or too many strangers), precedence of free over slave, right of another, lack of divine religion, conflict (master-slave), and triple divorce.

Ruling on Marrying a Daughter from Zina in Hanafi Fiqh
Explains prohibition of daughters from zina using Quranic text and introduces the principle of juz'iyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marrying a daughter born from zina, distinguish between the prohibition for the father and the permissibility for the uncle, and cite the Quranic proof and the principle of juz'iyya.

Core Idea
A daughter from zina is prohibited to her father due to linguistic inclusion; her uncle may marry her because juz'iyya is absent.

The Quranic prohibition of marrying one's 'daughters' seems straightforward, but complications arise for children born outside of marriage. In this lesson, we examine how the Hanafi school applies the general term 'بناتكم' to include biological daughters from zina, and how this prohibition extends to direct ascendants and descendants (based on juz'iyya) but not to collaterals like uncles. We'll also resolve an apparent contradiction within Hanafi texts regarding the uncle's position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Ruling
The matn states that a man's daughter (even from zina) is prohibited to him. Identify the act (marriage) and the ruling (haram, batil).
The ruling applies to the biological father regardless of the legitimacy of birth.
2
Locate the Quranic Dalil
The verse at 4:23 'حرمت عليكم أمهاتكم وبناتكم' is cited. Understand that the word 'بناتكم' is general in Arabic and no legal transfer (naql) has occurred to restrict it to legitimate daughters.
Contrast with 'salat' which was transferred from linguistic to legal meaning.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
The dalil proves the ruling through linguistic inclusion. The commentator explains that the address is in Arabic, so the literal meaning governs unless a legal transfer is established, which it is not.
This is a zanni dalala because the word could be classified as 'amm, but Hanafis consider 'amm as qat'i until takhsis is proven.
4
Note the Ikhtilaf about Relatives
The passage notes a contradiction: in the breastfeeding section, al-Bahr states the daughter from zina is not prohibited to the uncle (عم) because no lineage. Here, the ruling for the father is different. The reconciliation: prohibition on father is due to juz'iyya (partiality), which does not apply to the uncle.
The uncle can marry her according to Fath al-Qadir.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih and Mufta Bihi
The commentator sides with Fath al-Qadir, allowing the uncle to marry her. This becomes the operative position (mufta bihi).
The daughter of li'an is also prohibited to the man, but for a different reason (potential self-contradiction) or as a stepdaughter.

Source Text

And some say by the indication of the text that prohibits aunts and nieces; in the first, because the full siblings are children of grandmothers, so the prohibition of grandmothers, who are closer, is more appropriate, and in the second, because children's children are closer than siblings' children. And each of the explanations is correct. And it also contradicts what he mentioned in Fath al-Qadir there from al-Tajnis, where he said: It is not permissible for the fornicator to marry the nursing baby girl, nor for his father and grandfathers, nor for any of his children and their children, but the fornicator's paternal uncle may marry her. So if they produce a daughter, then she is prohibited to the brother, paternal uncle, maternal uncle, and grandfather. And the scenario in these issues is that he commits zina with a virgin and keeps her until she gives birth to a daughter, as in Fath al-Qadir from the discussion that zina necessitates marriage alliance. And the daughter of li'an also enters, so she has the ruling of a daughter here. If the judge negates her lineage from the man and assigns her to the mother, it is not permissible for the man to marry her because he is in a position to contradict himself and claim her, thus establishing his lineage. As in Fath al-Qadir. And we have previously mentioned in the chapter on disbursement from al-Mi'raj that the child of an umm walad whom he denied is not permissible to give zakat to him, and its implication is the establishment of daughterhood in matters based on precaution, so it is not permissible for his son to marry her because she is his sister out of precaution, and it depends on transmission. And it can be said about the daughter of li'an that she is prohibited because she is a stepchild and he has consummated with her mother, not because of what was forced in al-Fath, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and a scholarly dispute. It discusses the legal status of a daughter born from zina (adultery/fornication). The author of the matn (al-Kanz) stated that the prohibition of a daughter applies even if she is from zina, citing the general word 'daughters' in the Quranic verse. The commentator (Ibn Nujaym, author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) explains the reasoning: since 'daughter' is a linguistic term, and the Quranic address is in Arabic, it includes any biological daughter, regardless of the legitimacy of the birth. This is analogous to the word 'salat,' which was transferred to a legal meaning; but no such transfer exists for 'daughter' to restrict it to legitimate daughters. Therefore, a man cannot marry his own daughter from zina. The same applies to his sister from zina and other relatives like niece. However, the commentator notes an apparent contradiction: in the section on breastfeeding, it was stated that a daughter from zina is not prohibited to the fornicator's paternal uncle (عم) because her lineage is not established. The commentator reconciles by explaining that the prohibition on the father and his direct ascendants/descendants is due to 'partiality' (juz'iyya), meaning the child is part of the father's being, so the father cannot marry her. But the uncle has no such partiality, so the uncle can marry her. The text then cites Fath al-Qadir, which allows the uncle to marry the daughter from zina. The commentator also addresses the daughter of li'an (a child born of a woman who accuses her husband of impotence and is separated by li'an). Even though the judge negates the husband's lineage, he cannot marry her because he could later claim her, establishing lineage. Out of precaution, his children also cannot marry her, though this is based on transmission. The commentator suggests an alternative reasoning: she is prohibited as a stepdaughter (rabiba) because he has consummated the marriage with her mother.

Key Terms
بنت الزنا (bint al-zina): A daughter born from an illicit sexual relationship (zina), not from a valid marriage.
بنت الملاعنة (bint al-mula'anah): A daughter born to a woman who has been separated from her husband by li'an (mutual imprecation). Her lineage from the husband is negated by the judge, but she may still be prohibited in marriage to him due to precaution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, can a man marry his own daughter born from zina?
A) Yes, because her lineage is not legally established.
B) No, because she is his biological daughter linguistically.
C) Yes, but only with the permission of the judge.
D) No, because she is considered a stepdaughter.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the prohibition of marriage to a daughter from zina is based on the linguistic inclusion in the Quranic text and the principle of juz'iyya (partiality) for direct ascendants/descendants; the uncle is not prohibited from marrying her.

Marriage Prohibition: Daughter from Zina, Li'an, and Aunt Extensions
Commentary on two specific mahram extensions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marrying a daughter born from zina, explain the Quranic proof, and apply the distinction to determine the prohibition of the aunt of the aunt.

Core Idea
A daughter from zina is a mahram; the aunt of the aunt is prohibited only if the closer aunt is from the father's side.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq expands on the list of women prohibited for marriage due to blood relationship. It tackles two nuanced cases: first, whether a daughter born from a man's own act of zina falls under the Quranic prohibition of 'your daughters'; second, the detailed ruling on the paternal aunt of one's paternal aunt (ammat al-ammah), which depends on the lineage of the closer aunt. The Hanafi school provides clear proofs and distinctions that resolve apparent ambiguities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key rulings
Read the commentary to identify the two main legal rulings: (1) a daughter born from zina is prohibited to the father, brother, uncles, and grandfather; (2) the paternal aunt of a paternal aunt is prohibited only if the closer aunt is from the father's side.
These rulings are extensions from the general Quranic prohibition and the principle of blood lineage.
2
Locate the dalil for the zina ruling
The commentator cites the Quranic verse (4:23) 'your daughters' as the primary proof. Note that the Hanafi school considers this general term inclusive of daughters from zina because zina creates affinity (musahara).
The Shafi'i school does not accept this extension due to lack of established lineage.
3
Understand the aunt extension distinction
For the aunt of the aunt, the decisive factor is whether the closer aunt is a sister of the father (paternal aunt from father's side) or a sister of the mother (paternal aunt from mother's side). Only the former extends prohibition to her aunt.
The reasoning: if the closer aunt is from the father, her paternal aunt is the sister of the grandfather, who is a mahram; if from the mother, her father is the husband of the grandmother, and his sister is not a blood relative.
4
Note the textual correction in al-Khaniyah
The commentator corrects a slip in some copies of al-Khaniyah that said 'li-ab' (from father) to 'li-umm' (from mother) for the non-prohibition case.
This highlights the importance of verifying transmitted texts.

Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn from Kanz al-Daqaiq regarding women prohibited due to blood relationship. The author first addresses a specific case: a female child born from zina. The general principle is that a man cannot marry his daughter, but what about a daughter born from his own act of zina? The commentator clarifies that she is indeed prohibited to him and to his close male relatives (brother, uncle, grandfather) because zina, though sinful, does establish affinity (musahara) according to the Hanafi school, as transmitted from Fath al-Qadir. The ruling also applies to a daughter born from li'an (mutual cursing) where the father denies paternity; even if the court attaches her to the mother, he cannot marry her because he has the ability to later claim her. The commentator then moves to the standard list: his sister, her daughter (i.e. his sister's daughter), his brother's daughter, his paternal aunt, his maternal aunt. The commentary clarifies that these designations include all half-siblings and their descendants, and all aunts even those ascending through grandfathers and grandmothers. A key sub-discussion involves the ruling on 'the aunt of the aunt' (ammat al-ammah). If the closer aunt is from both parents or from the father's side, then her aunt (i.e. the sister of the grandfather) is also prohibited. If the closer aunt is only from the mother's side, then her aunt is not prohibited. This differentiation is based on the principle that the prohibition only extends through the father's line for paternal aunts. The commentator also notes a correction to a copy of al-Khaniyah where it said 'li-ab' (from father) but should be 'li-umm' (from mother). The passage concludes by affirming that the daughter from zina is included in the Quranic verse of prohibited women.

Key Terms
المصاهرة (al-musāharah): Affinity; a relationship created by marriage that establishes certain prohibitions. In this text, the author argues that zina also creates musahara for the purpose of prohibiting marriage with the child born from that zina.
الملاعنة (al-mulā'anah): The mutual cursing between spouses when the husband accuses the wife of adultery and she denies it. The resulting child is attached to the mother and not the husband, but still considered prohibited in marriage to the accuser.
عمة العمة (ammat al-ammah): The paternal aunt of one's paternal aunt. The ruling on her prohibition depends on whether the closer aunt is from the father's side or mother's side.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, a daughter born from zina is prohibited in marriage to which of the following?
A) Only her father and brother
B) Her father, brother, paternal uncle, maternal uncle, and grandfather
C) Only her father and paternal uncle
D) She is not prohibited because lineage is not established

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school expands marriage prohibitions to include daughters from zina and applies a lineage-based condition to the prohibition of the aunt of the aunt.

Prohibition Due to Affinity: Mother of Wife and Stepdaughter
Commentary on the prohibition of marrying the mother of wife (without entry condition) and stepdaughter (with entry condition) with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for the prohibition of the mother of one's wife and stepdaughter, and apply the Hanafi rules to determine when each becomes haram.

Core Idea
Mother of wife: haram immediately. Stepdaughter: haram only after consummation with her mother.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains two key prohibitions from affinity: the mother of one's wife and the stepdaughter. Both are based on Quran 4:23, but their conditions differ. The Hanafi school clarifies that the mother is prohibited upon the marriage contract itself, while the stepdaughter requires entry (or its equivalent) with the mother. The text also addresses nuances like invalid marriage and the status of grandmothers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Distinguish the mother of wife (unconditional prohibition) from the stepdaughter (conditional on entry). Note the difference in the Quranic text.
2
Locate the primary proof
Both rulings are derived from Quran 4:23. For the mother, the verse is general; for the stepdaughter, it includes the phrase 'dakhaltum bihinna' (you have entered with them).
3
Analyze the proof for mother of wife
The phrase 'ummahāt nisā'ikum' is unconditional. The commentator notes consensus that prohibition takes effect upon the valid marriage contract itself, regardless of consummation.
4
Analyze the proof for stepdaughter
The verse conditions prohibition on having 'entered' with the mother. The commentator explains that 'dakhaltum bihinna' is a metaphor for sexual intercourse, and that 'hijr' (care) is incidental, not a condition.
5
Understand Hanafi interpretation of 'entry'
Abu Hanifah holds that touching or looking with desire stands in place of actual intercourse. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; the mufta bihi is that prohibition is established by any means equivalent to entry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب النكاح فصل في المحرمات
امرأته وبنتها إن دخل بها وامرأة أبيه وابنه وإن بعدا والكل رضاعاً والجمع بين الأختين
أن لا تحرم، وأما خالة الخالة فإن كانت الخالة القربي خالة لأب وأم أو لأم فخالتها تحرم عليه، فإن كانت القربي خالة لأب فخالتها لا تحرم عليه لأن أم الخالة القربى تكون امرأة الجد أبي الأم لا أم أمه وأختها تكون أخت امرأة أبي الأم وأخت امرأة الجد لا تحرم عليه الد. وكما يحرم على الرجل أن يتزوج بمن ذكر يحرم على المرأة التزوج بنظير من ذكر . وعبارة النقاية أولى وهي: وحرم أصله أي التزوج ذكراً كان أو أنثى ، وفرعه وفرع أصله القريب وصلبية أصله البعيد قوله : (وأم امرأته بيان لما ثبت بالمصاهرة لقوله تعالى وأمهات نسائكم [النساء : ٢٣] أطلقه فلا فرق بين كون امرأته مدخولاً بها أو لا، وهو مجمع عليه عند الأئمة الأربعة، وتوضيحه في الكشاف. ويدخل في لفظ الأمهات جداتها من قبل أبيها وأمها وإن علون. وقيد بالمرأة فانصرف إلى النكاح الصحيح فإن تزوجها فاسداً فلا تحرم أمها بمجرد العقد بل بالوطء أو ما يقوم مقامه من المس بشهوة والنظر بشهوة لأن الإضافة لا تثبت إلا بالعقد الصحيح، وإن كانت أمته فلا تحرم أمها إلا بالوطء أو دواعيه لأن لفظ النساء إذا أضيف إلى الأزواج كان المراد منه الحرائر كما في الظهار والإيلاء قوله : (وبنتها أن دخل بها) لقوله تعالى وربائبكم اللاتي في حجوركم من نسائكم اللاتي دخلتم بهن فإن لم تكونوا دخلتم بهن فلا جناج عليكم [النساء : ۲۳] قال في الكشاف : فإن قلت ما معنى «دخلتم بهن»؟ قلت: هو كناية عن الجماع كقولهم بنى عليها وضرب عليها الحجاب. وذكر الحجر في الآية خرج مخرج العادة أو ذكر للتشنيع عليهم لا لتعلق الحكم به نحو أضعاف مضاعفة» في قوله تعالى لا تأكلوا الربا أضعافاً مضاعفة ﴾ [آل عمران : ۱۳۰] اهـ وتفسير الحجر أن تزف البنت مع الأم إلى بيت زوج الأم، وأما إذا كانت البنت مع الأب لم تكن في حجر زوج الأم. وفي المغرب: حجر الإنسان بالفتح والكسر حضنه وهو ما دون إبطه إلى الكشح . ثم قالوا : فلان في حجرة فلان أي في كنفه ومنعته كما في الآية اهـ . وأما بنات الربيبة وبنات أبنائها وإن سفلن فتثبت حرمتهن بالإجماع وبما ذكرنا أولاً وفي الكشاف واللمس ونحوه يقوم مقام الدخول عند أبي حنيفة. وفي
The Book of Nikah, Chapter on Prohibited Women. His wife and her daughter if he has entered with her, and the wife of his father and his son even if distant, and all through rada'ah, and combining two sisters. That she be not prohibited. As for the maternal aunt of the maternal aunt: if the closer maternal aunt is a maternal aunt from father and mother or from mother only, then her maternal aunt is prohibited upon him. If the closer maternal aunt is a maternal aunt from father only, then her maternal aunt is not prohibited upon him, because the mother of the closer maternal aunt is the wife of the grandfather, the father of the mother, not the mother of the mother, and her sister is the sister of the wife of the father of the mother, and the sister of the wife of the grandfather is not prohibited upon him. Just as it is prohibited for a man to marry those mentioned, it is prohibited for a woman to marry the counterpart of those mentioned. The expression of al-Niqayah is more appropriate: 'He prohibits his origin, i.e., marriage, whether male or female, and his branch, and the branch of his close origin, and the direct line of his far origin.' He said: 'And the mother of his wife' is an explanation of what is established by affinity, due to His saying 'and the mothers of your wives' (Quran 4:23). He made it general, so there is no difference whether his wife is entered with or not. This is unanimously agreed upon by the four Imams, and its clarification is in al-Kashshaf. Included in the word 'mothers' are her grandmothers from her father's side and her mother's side even if high. It is restricted to the wife, so it applies to a valid marriage. If he marries her invalidly, then her mother is not prohibited by mere contract, but only by intercourse or what stands in its place such as touching with desire and looking with desire, because the relation is only established by a valid contract. And if she is his slave, then her mother is not prohibited except by intercourse or its precursors, because the word 'women' when added to husbands means free women, as in zihar and ila'. He said: 'And her daughter if he has entered with her' due to His saying 'and your stepdaughters who are in your care from your wives to whom you have entered; but if you have not entered to them, there is no sin upon you' (Quran 4:23). He said in al-Kashshaf: If you ask 'what is the meaning of 'dakhaltum bihinna'?' I replied: It is a metaphor for sexual intercourse, like saying 'he built on her' and 'he struck the veil on her'. The mention of 'care' in the verse is mentioned out of habit or to rebuke them, not that the ruling is attached to it, like 'doubled multiplication' in His saying 'do not consume usury, doubled and multiplied' (Quran 3:130). End. The interpretation of 'care' is that the daughter is brought with the mother to the husband's house. But if the daughter is with the father, she is not in the care of the husband. In al-Mughrib: 'hajr' of a person (with fat-h and kasr) is his bosom, which is the area under his armpit to the flank. Then they said: so-and-so is in the care of so-and-so, i.e., in his protection and guardianship, as in the verse. End. As for the daughters of the stepdaughter and the daughters of her sons even if lower, their prohibition is established by consensus and by what we mentioned first. In al-Kashshaf, touching and the like stand in place of entry according to Abu Hanifah. And in...
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage continues the chapter on prohibited women in marriage. It begins by completing the discussion on the maternal aunt (khala) of a maternal aunt, applying the same logic as with the paternal aunt. The key point is that if the closer maternal aunt is from the father's side only, her maternal aunt is not prohibited; otherwise, she is. The text then moves to the prohibitions due to affinity. First, the mother of one's wife is prohibited immediately upon marriage, regardless of whether the marriage was consummated, based on the Quranic verse 'and the mothers of your wives' (4:23). This is a consensus among the four schools. The prohibition includes all grandmothers of the wife. However, if the marriage to the wife is invalid (fasid), the mother is not prohibited by mere contract but only by actual intercourse or its equivalents (touching/looking with desire). If the wife is a slave, the prohibition of her mother also requires intercourse. Next, the stepdaughter (daughter of one's wife) is prohibited only if the marriage with her mother was consummated, based on the Quranic verse 'and your stepdaughters who are in your care from your wives to whom you have entered' (4:23). The commentator clarifies that 'dakhaltum bihinna' is a metaphor for sexual intercourse, and the mention of 'hijr' (care) is incidental, not a condition. Thus, if the stepdaughter is with the mother in the husband's house or elsewhere, as long as he has entered with the mother, she is prohibited. Abu Hanifah holds that touching and the like stand in place of actual entry for this ruling. The prohibition extends to step-granddaughters as well.

Key Terms
المصاهرة (al-musāharah): Affinity; relationship through marriage that creates prohibition. Here it applies to the mother of wife and stepdaughter.
الربائب (al-rabā'ib): Stepdaughters; daughters of one's wife from another marriage. They are prohibited only if the husband has entered with the mother.
الحجر (al-hijr): Care, bosom, or protection. In the Quranic context, it refers to the stepdaughter being under the care of the husband, but it is not a condition for prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Immediately upon marrying the mother
B) Only if the marriage with the mother was consummated through intercourse or its equivalent
C) Only if the mother was married with a valid contract and the stepdaughter lived in the husband's house
D) She is never prohibited if she is from a previous marriage

Key Takeaway
The mother of one's wife is haram immediately upon the marriage contract, while the stepdaughter becomes haram only after consummation (or its equivalent) with the mother, based on the explicit wording of Quran 4:23.

Prohibition of Wife of Father: Contract vs. Intercourse
Establishing prohibition by Quran and debating interpretation of 'nikah'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the prohibition of marrying the wife of one's father and explain the debate over the meaning of 'nikah' in Quran 4:22.

Core Idea
A woman becomes prohibited to the son by the father's mere marriage contract — no consummation needed.

The Quran clearly prohibits marrying women who were married to one's father. However, the precise trigger of this prohibition is debated: does it require the father to have consummated the marriage, or does the contract alone suffice? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks the evidence and the Hanafi position that the contract is decisive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act is marrying the wife of one's father (and by extension, son). The hukm is haram (prohibited).
The prohibition extends to grandfathers and grandsons (and even if distant).
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is Quran 4:22: 'And do not marry what your fathers married of women.' The word 'nikah' here is key.
The verse is qat'i al-thubut but zanni al-dalala due to ambiguity of 'nikah'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does this verse prove the ruling? The imperative 'la tankihu' (do not marry) commands abstaining from marriage contracts. Therefore, the prohibition applies to women who were subject to a marriage contract by the father.
Hanafis argue that 'nikah' in this context means the marriage contract, not intercourse.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some scholars (e.g., Shafi'is) interpret 'nikah' as intercourse, so the prohibition only applies if the father had sexual relations. This changes the scope of the ruling.
The practical difference: if the father married but never consummated, the son may or may not be permitted, depending on the interpretation.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi school adheres to the contract-based prohibition, supported by consensus and the evident meaning of the verse. The mufta bihi position is that mere contract renders the woman haram.
The author notes that even the metaphorical interpretation (including both contract and intercourse) requires evidence, but the default is contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وامرأة أبيه وابنه وإن بعدا أما حليلة الأب فبقوله تعالى ولا تنكحوا ما نكح آباؤكم النساء﴾ [النساء : ۲۲] فتحرم بمجرد العقد عليها والآية المذكورة استدل بها المشايخ كصاحب النهاية وغيره على ثبوت حرمة المصاهرة بالزنا بناء على إرادة الوطء بالنكاح، فإن أريد به حرمة امرأة الأب والجد ما يطابقها من إرادة الوطء قصر عن إفادة تمام الحكم المطلوب حيث قال : ولا بامرأة أبيه . وتصدق امرأة الأب بعقده عليها وإلا لم يفد الحكم في ذلك المحل. وإنما يصح على اعتبار لفظ النكاح في نكاح الآباء في معنى مجازي يعم ا العقد والوطء ولك النظر في تعيينه ويحتاج إلى دليل يوجب اعتبارها في المجازي
His statement: (and the wife of his father and his son, even if distant). As for the wife of the father, by His statement: 'And do not marry what your fathers married of women' [An-Nisa 4:22]; so she is prohibited by mere contract upon her. And the mentioned verse was used as evidence by the mashāyikh, such as the author of an-Nihāyah and others, for the establishment of the prohibition of affinity (muṣāharah) through fornication, based on intending intercourse by 'nikāḥ'. If what is intended by the prohibition of the wife of the father and grandfather is what corresponds to it from the intention of intercourse, it falls short of conveying the complete desired ruling, as He said: 'and not the wife of his father'. And the wife of the father is verified by his contract upon her; otherwise it would not convey the ruling in that place. And it is only valid on the consideration of the word 'nikāḥ' in the 'nikāḥ of fathers' in a metaphorical sense that encompasses both contract and intercourse, but you need to examine its specification and it requires evidence necessitating its consideration in the metaphorical sense.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (ḥukm bi-dalīl) from the commentary on Kanz ad-Daqāʾiq. The author explains the prohibition of marrying the wife of one's father (or grandfather) based on Quran 4:22. The verse literally says 'do not marry what your fathers married of women' — the word 'nikāḥ' here is interpreted by the early masters (mashāyikh) as meaning intercourse (waṭʾ), so that the prohibition extends to any woman with whom the father had intercourse, even through fornication. However, the immediate context of the verse focuses on contract; the verse is addressing marriage contracts. The author notes that if 'nikāḥ' is taken solely as intercourse, it would not cover the wife of the father who was married by contract but never consummated, which contradicts the established ruling that she is prohibited by mere contract. Hence, the preferred interpretation is that 'nikāḥ' here is used in a metaphorical sense that includes both contract and intercourse, but this requires evidence. The passage sets up a dispute about the correct interpretation, which continues in the next lesson.

Key Terms
نكاح (nikāḥ): In this context, it is debated whether it refers to the marriage contract (عقد) or intercourse (وطء). The Hanafi school leans towards contract based on usage in the verse.
حليلة (ḥalīlah): Wife; literally 'she who is lawful for the husband' or 'she who settles in the bed'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Immediately upon the father's marriage contract
C) Only if the son himself consummates
D) It is never prohibited

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a woman becomes permanently prohibited (haram) to the son by the father's valid marriage contract alone, regardless of consummation.

Combining Literal and Metaphorical Meanings in Negation
Refuting the claim that combining literal and metaphorical meanings is allowed in negation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi position on combining literal and metaphorical meanings in negation and apply it to the interpretation of the word 'nikāḥ' in Quran 4:22.

Core Idea
Combining literal and metaphorical meanings in negation is not permitted; 'nikāḥ' means contract only.

This passage addresses a key usul dispute: can a word simultaneously convey its literal and metaphorical senses when used in a negation? Imam az-Zaylaʿī argued that negation is an exception, allowing such combination, but the majority of Hanafi scholars reject this. The debate directly impacts the interpretation of Quran 4:22, which prohibits marrying 'what your fathers married'. The verse's meaning determines whether it covers both marriage contract and illicit intercourse. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to analyse usul arguments and their fiqh consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify az-Zaylaʿī's Claim
Locate his argument: the verse includes both contract and intercourse because it is a negation, which permits combining literal and metaphorical meanings, similar to homonyms in negation.
Az-Zaylaʿī is a prominent Hanafi scholar; his view is considered weak here.
2
Understand the Hanafi Rebuttal
The author declares his view weak and asserts that combining is not allowed in either negation or affirmation. Al-Akmal adds that the permissibility depends on affirmation—if affirmation wouldn't allow combining, negation doesn't either.
The principle is that negation mirrors affirmation in this regard.
3
Analyse the Oath Example from al-Mabsūṭ
The example: one swears not to speak to his 'mawlā', and he has higher and lower masters. Speaking to any breaks the oath. The author clarifies this is not due to combining meanings in negation, but because the context shifts the meaning to a metaphor encompassing all types (those to whom manumission pertains).
This counters the claim that the example supports combination.
4
Synthesise the Conclusion for the Verse
The correct usul leads to the consensus that 'nikāḥ' in the verse means contract only. The prohibition of intercourse with the father's wife (outside marriage) comes from another evidence.
Thus, the verse alone does not prohibit fornication with the father's wife; that ruling is derived elsewhere.
5
Apply the Practical Ruling from al-Muḥīṭ
If a man claims intercourse with his slave-girl, she becomes forbidden to his son. But if the slave-girl belongs to another, the son may disregard the claim due to apparent circumstances. Also, purchasing a slave-girl from the father's inheritance requires waiting until the father's intercourse is known.
These rulings show the practical impact of the usul dispute on inheritance and marriage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقول الزيلعي «إن الآية تتناول منكوحة الأب وطئاً وعقداً صحيحاً وإن كان فيه جمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز لأنه نفي، وفي النفي يجوز الجمع بينهما كما يجوز في المشترك أن . معانيه في النفي» اهـ . ضعيف في الأصول والصحيح أنه لا يجوز الجمع بينهما لا في النفي ولا في الإثبات ولا عموم للمشترك مطلقاً . قال الأكمل في التقرير : والحق أن النفي لما اقتضاه الإثبات فإن اقتضى الإثبات الجمع بين المعنيين فالنفي كذلك وإلا فلا . وأما مسألة اليمين المذكورة في المبسوط : حلف لا يكلم مولاك وله أعلون وأسفلون أيهم كلم حنث . فليس باعتبار عموم المشترك في النفي كما توهمه البعض وإنما هو لأن حقيقة الكلام متروكة بدلالة اليمين إلى مجاز يعمهما وهو أن يكون الموالي من تعلق به عتق وهو بعمومه يتناول الأعلى والأسفل .اهـ لكن اختار المحقق في التحرير أنه يعم في النفي لأنه نكرة في النفي والمنفي ما سمي باللفظ وتمام تحقيقة في الأصول. فالحاصل أن الأولى أن النكاح في الآية للعقد كما هو المجمع عليه، ويستدل لثبوت حرمة المصاهرة بالوطء الحرام بدليل آخر . وفي المحيط : رجل له جارية فقال قد وطئتها لا تحل لابنه ، وإن كانت في غير ملكه فقال قد وطئتها يحل لابنه أن يكذبه ويطأها لأن الظاهر يشهد له ولو اشترى جارية من ميراث أبيه يسـ أن يطأها حتى يعلم أن الأب وطئها
And the statement of az-Zaylaʿī: 'The verse includes the father's married woman by intercourse and by valid contract, even if there is combining of literal (ḥaqīqah) and metaphorical (majāz) because it is negation (nafy), and in negation combining them is permitted, just as in a homonym it is permitted to combine its meanings in negation.' End. Weak in uṣūl, and the correct view is that it is not permissible to combine them, neither in negation nor in affirmation, and there is no generality for a homonym absolutely. Al-Akmal said in at-Taqrīr: The truth is that negation requires affirmation; if affirmation necessitates combining the two meanings, then negation likewise, otherwise not. As for the case of the oath mentioned in al-Mabsūṭ: one swore he would not speak to his mawlā, and he has higher and lower mawālī; whichever he speaks to, he breaks his oath. That is not by considering the generality of the homonym in negation, as some imagined, but rather because the literal meaning of the statement is abandoned due to the indication of the oath to a metaphor that encompasses both, which is that the mawlā is the one to whom manumission pertains, and in its generality it includes the higher and lower. End. But al-Muḥaqqiq in at-Taḥrīr chose that it is general in negation because it is a nakirah in the context of negation, and the negated is what is named by the word. The full verification is in uṣūl. So the conclusion is that the preferred view is that 'nikāḥ' in the verse means the contract, as is agreed upon, and the prohibition of affinity through unlawful intercourse is established by another evidence. And in al-Muḥīṭ: A man who had a slave-girl said, 'I have had intercourse with her'; she is not permissible for his son. And if she was in someone else's possession and he said, 'I have had intercourse with her', his son is permitted to disbelieve him and have intercourse with her because the apparent circumstance supports him. And if he buys a slave-girl from his father's inheritance, he may not have intercourse with her until he knows that his father had intercourse with her.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (ḥāshiyah) within the commentary. The author critiques the view of Imam az-Zaylaʿī that in a negation (like the verse 'do not marry what your fathers married'), it is permissible to combine the literal meaning (contract) and the metaphorical meaning (intercourse) because negation allows combining multiple meanings of a homonym. The author declares this weak in uṣūl al-fiqh, stating that combining literal and metaphorical is not allowed in either negation or affirmation. He supports this with al-Akmal's argument that the permissibility depends on whether affirmation would allow combining. The author then discusses an example from al-Mabsūṭ about an oath regarding 'mawlā' — a homonym that can mean higher or lower master. The example shows that the oath is broken by speaking to any mawlā, but that is not due to combining meanings in negation; rather, the context (oath) shifts the meaning to a metaphor that covers both. The author notes that al-Muḥaqqiq (Ibn al-Humām) in at-Taḥrīr holds that in negation, a nakirah (indefinite noun) is general, so the word 'mawlā' in negation would include all types. The conclusion for the main issue: the word 'nikāḥ' in the verse should be interpreted as contract (the agreed-upon meaning), and the prohibition of affinity through fornication is established by separate evidence. The passage ends with a practical ruling from al-Muḥīṭ: if a man claims to have had intercourse with his slave-girl, she becomes forbidden to his son, but if the slave-girl is owned by another and the man claims intercourse, the son may disregard it based on apparent circumstances. Also, if one buys a slave-girl from his father's inheritance, he must wait until he knows whether the father had intercourse with her.

Key Terms
نفي (nafy): Negation; a grammatical form that denies a proposition. In usul, the treatment of nafy differs from affirmative in terms of scope.
الحقيقة والمجاز (al-ḥaqīqah wa al-majāz): Literal and metaphorical meanings of a word. The debate is whether they can be combined in a single use, especially in negation.
مشترك (mushtarak): Homonym; a word with multiple distinct meanings (e.g., 'mawlā' meaning master or slave).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct Hanafi usul position presented in this passage, is it permissible to combine the literal and metaphorical meanings of a word in a negation?
A) Yes, because negation is an exception.
B) No, it is not permissible in either negation or affirmation.
C) Yes, but only for homonyms.
D) No, unless the context is an oath.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, combining literal and metaphorical meanings is not allowed in negation; thus 'nikāḥ' in Quran 4:22 means the marriage contract only.

The Prohibition of the Son's Wife: Hanafi Interpretation of Quran 4:23
Quranic basis and scope of prohibition of son's wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi interpretation of 'halila' in Quran 4:23 and apply it to identify which women are permanently prohibited to a father.

Core Idea
The wife of one's son is absolutely prohibited; the Hanafi school extends this to any woman with whom the son had intercourse.

The prohibition of marrying the wife of one's son is established in Quran 4:23. However, the scope of the term 'halila' has been a point of interpretation. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position, which broadens the prohibition beyond the lawful wife to include any woman with whom the son has had intimate relations, whether through marriage, ownership, or even fornication. Understanding this expansion is crucial for correctly applying the rulings of permanent prohibition in Islamic family law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic text
Locate the verse (Quran 4:23) and the specific phrase 'wa hala'il abna'ikum alladhina min aslabikum'. Note that the key term is 'halail' (plural of halila).
The verse explicitly prohibits marrying the wives of one's sons from one's own loins.
2
Analyze the term 'halila' according to Hanafi principles
Understand that Hanafis derive the meaning from the root 'hall' (to untie or make lawful), interpreting it broadly to include any woman with whom the son has had sexual intercourse, whether through marriage, concubinage, or even fornication.
This is based on the idea that 'halila' is related to 'hull' (loosening the waistcloth) or 'hulul' (alighting in the bed).
3
Determine the scope of prohibited women
Apply the Hanafi interpretation: the prohibition covers the son's wife, his concubine, a woman he had sex with under a mistaken belief (shubha), and even a fornicatress (if proved). It also extends to the wives of grandsons and adopted sons are excluded by 'min aslabikum'.
The phrase 'min aslabikum' excludes adopted sons, restricting the prohibition to biological descendants.
4
Compare with the Shafi'i view
Note that the Shafi'i school restricts 'halila' solely to the lawful wife, based on the lexical definition in works like al-Mughrib. The Hanafi view relies on additional proofs (other hadiths or qiyas) to establish prohibition for non-wives.
This difference leads to practical variations in who is considered permanently prohibited to the father.
5
Evaluate the practical consequence (thamarah)
Under Hanafi law, a father cannot marry any woman his son ever had intercourse with, even if the son married her, divorced her before consummation, or if the relationship was illicit. Under Shafi'i law, only the son's lawful wife (and only after consummation) is prohibited.
This affects cases like: if a son commits zina with a woman, Hanafi forbids the father from marrying her; Shafi'i does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالحاصل أن الأولى أن النكاح في الآية للعقد كما هو المجمع عليه، ويستدل لثبوت حرمة المصاهرة بالوطء الحرام بدليل آخر . وأما حليلة الابن فبقوله تعالى ﴿وحلائل أبنائكم الذين من أصلابكم﴾ [النساء : ٢٣] فإن اعتبرت الحليلة من حلول الفراش أو حل الإزار تناولت الموطوءة بملك اليمين أو شبهة أو زنى فيحرم الكل على الآباء وهو الحكم الثابت عندنا، ولا يتناول المعقود عليها للابن أو بنيه وإن سفلوا قبل الوطء، والفرض أنها بمجرد العقد تحرم على الآباء وذلك باعتباره من الحل - بكسر الحاء - وقد قام الدليل على حرمة المزني بها للابن على الأب فيجب اعتباره في أعم من الحل والحل . ثم يراد بالأبناء الفروع فتحرم حليلة الابن السافل على الجد الأعلى، وكذا حليلة ابن البنت وإن سفل، وكما تحرم حليلة الابن من النسب تحرم حليلة الابن من الرضاع. وذكر الأصلاب في الآية لإسقاط حليلة الابن المتبني . كذا في فتح القدير والظاهر أن الحليلة الزوجة كما في المغرب فتحرم زوجة الابن على الأب مطلقاً بالآية، وأما حرمة من وطئها ممن ليس بزوجة فبدليل آخر، وكونها من حلول الفراش لا يقتضي تناولها للموطوءة بملك اليمين وغيره بل لا بد من قيد الزوجية، فإن صاحب المغرب فسرها بالزوجة ثم قال : لأنها تحل زوجها في فراش .
So the conclusion is that the first (view) is that marriage in the verse refers to the contract, as is agreed upon. And evidence is sought for the establishment of the prohibition of affinity by unlawful intercourse through another proof. As for the wife of the son, it is by His statement: 'And the wives of your sons from your loins' [4:23]. If 'al-halilah' is considered from 'al-hulul' (alighting) of the bed or 'hall al-izar' (loosening the waistcloth), it includes the one with whom intercourse occurred by right of ownership, doubtful intercourse, or fornication, so all of them are forbidden to fathers, and that is the established ruling with us. It does not include the one contracted in marriage for the son or his grandsons, even if lower, before intercourse. The assumption is that she becomes forbidden to fathers by mere contract, and that is by considering it from 'al-hill' (lawfulness) with a kasra. And proof has been established for the prohibition of the woman with whom the son committed fornication upon the father, so it is necessary to consider it in the more general sense of 'al-hill' and 'al-hull'. Then by 'sons' branches are meant, so the wife of the lower son is forbidden to the upper grandfather, and likewise the wife of the son's daughter, even if lower. And just as the wife of the son by lineage is forbidden, so is the wife of the son by fosterage. And the mention of 'from your loins' in the verse is to exclude the wife of the adopted son. Such is in Fath al-Qadir. And the apparent meaning is that 'al-halilah' is the wife, as in al-Mughrib, so the wife of the son is absolutely forbidden to the father by the verse. As for the prohibition of the one with whom intercourse occurred who is not a wife, it is by another proof. And that it is from 'hulul al-firash' does not necessitate that it includes the one with whom intercourse occurred by right of ownership or other; rather, the condition of marriage is necessary. For the author of al-Mughrib interpreted it as the wife, then said: 'Because she makes her husband lawful in his bed.'
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Quran. It explains the Hanafi position on the prohibition of the wife of the son (including by fosterage) based on Quran 4:23. The commentator discusses the meaning of 'halila' (spouse) and whether it includes those with whom intercourse occurred unlawfully or by ownership. The Hanafi school holds that the verse covers all forms of intercourse (ownership, doubt, fornication) for purposes of prohibition, but the prohibition of the son's wife is established by the verse, while prohibition of non-spouses established by other evidence. The passage also clarifies that 'sons' includes grandsons, and adopted sons are excluded by the phrase 'min aslabikum'.

Key Terms
حليلة (halila): The wife or lawful sexual partner of a man; in this context, it is interpreted broadly to include women with whom intercourse occurs through ownership or fornication.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Only the wife of the son

C) The wife of the son and any woman with whom the son has had intercourse
D) Only the wife of the son by lineage

Key Takeaway
The prohibition of the son's wife is absolute, and in Hanafi fiqh it extends to any woman with whom the son had sexual relations, based on the broad interpretation of 'halila' in Quran 4:23.
